لفظياً.
الله فيكون عين المسمى وكلا الاستعمالين ثابت كما في قولك الأسماء والأفعال والحروف، وقوله تعالى : سبح اسم ربك ﴾ [الأعلى : ١] وتبارك اسم ربك [الرحمن : ۷۸]، أي مسماه. وقول لبيد : ثم اسم السلام عليكما. لكن هذا بحث لغوي لا فائدة فيه هاهنا . وقال الإمام الرازي: المشهور عن أصحابنا أن الاسم هو اسم المسمى وعن المعتزلة أنه التسمية وعن ) الغزالي أنه مغاير لهما لأن النسبة وطرفيها متغايرة قطعا والناس قد طولوا في هذه المسألة وهو عندي فضول لأن الاسم هو المخصوص والمسمى ما وضع ذلك اللفظ بإزائه فنقول : الاسم قد يكون غير المسمى، فإن لفظة الجدار مغايرة لحقيقة الجدار وقد يكون عينه، فإن لفظ اسم للفظ الدال على المعنى المجرد عن الزمان، ومن جملة الألفاظ لفظ الاسم فيكون لفظ الاسم اسماً لنفسه، فاتحد هاهنا الاسم أو المسمى، قال : فهذا ما عندي في هذه المسألة
The Seventh Observatory - First Purpose: The name is not the act of naming. The self-subsistent (neither He nor other as previously, so likewise the case for the essence taken with that attribute). Al-Amidi said: The rational people agreed on the distinction between the act of naming (tasmīya) and the named (musammā). Most of our companions held that the act of naming is the very indicative utterances, and that the name (ism) is the very indicated meaning. Then these differed: Ibn Furak and others held that every name is identical to the named itself. Thus your saying 'Allah' is an utterance indicating a name which is the named, and likewise your saying 'Knower' and 'Creator' indicates the Lord described as being knowing and creating. And some said: Among names, some are identical like 'existent' and 'essence', and some are other like 'creator', for the named is His essence and the name is the very creation, and His creation is other than His essence. And among them is that which is neither identical nor other like 'knower', for the named is His essence and the name is His knowledge, which is neither identical to His essence nor other than it. The Mu'tazila held that the name is the act of naming, and they were followed in this by some later scholars among our companions. And the master Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub held that the word 'name' is equivocal between the act of naming and the named, so it is applied to each and the intended meaning is understood by context. It is not hidden from you that the dispute according to Abu Nasr is only about the word 'name' and that it is applied to words, so the act of naming is in the mentioned sense, not in the sense of the act of the positor, or it is applied to their meanings. The name is identical. His saying: (So likewise the case for the essence taken with that attribute). Apparently what is meant by essence is the essence of the Necessary, and in it is that the concept of knowledge is an essence having knowledge, not the essence of the Necessary, exalted is He, unless what is meant is the absolute essence, or it is said: there is a dominance in it. Absolutely it is. His saying: (So Ibn Furak etc.) Clarification of the matter: Ibn Furak and those who agreed with him applied the name to the literal signification of the word. If by the named they meant that for which the name is posited, then the meaning of the statement that the name is the named itself is apparent. If they meant that to which the name is applied, namely the essence, as indicated by the commentator's statement 'it indicates the essence etc.', then they intend by the name being identical to the named the unity considered in predication and partiality. Those who opposed him intended by the named the second meaning and took the signification to be more general than the literal. They considered in the names of attributes the intended meanings, so they claimed that the signification of 'creator' is creation and that it is other than the essence of the Creator, based on what is in general matters: that attributes of actions are other than the described, and that attributes which are neither identical nor other are those whose separation from their subject is impossible. As for al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, he intended by the name its literal signification and by the named the essence. So the literal signification of the essential name is identical to the named. For something like 'creator' and 'provider', it is other. For 'knower' and 'powerful', it is neither identical nor other. So there is no need for what the commentator of al-Maqasid mentioned to explain his statement. And this is how we explained the statement of some who opposed Ibn Furak, due to their explicit statement that the name is the creation itself, unless al-Shaykh's explicit statement to that effect is also established. His saying: (like 'existent') is based on existence being identical to essence. Which are. His saying: (It is the act of naming and it is other than the named). It is not hidden that making the name identical to the act of naming is clearly false, unless a convention is established, and then the dispute with them would be terminological. His saying: (in the mentioned sense) i.e., the indicative utterance. Verbally. Allah, so it becomes identical to the named, and both usages are established, as in your saying 'nouns, verbs, and particles', and His saying, exalted: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (Q. 87:1) and 'Blessed be the name of your Lord' (Q. 55:78), i.e., His named. And the saying of Labid: 'Then the name of peace be upon you both.' But this is a linguistic discussion with no benefit here. Imam al-Razi said: The well-known from our companions is that the name is the name of the named, and from the Mu'tazila that it is the act of naming, and from al-Ghazali that it is other than both because the relation and its two terms are necessarily distinct. People have gone on at length about this issue, and in my view it is superfluous, because the name is the particular thing and the named is that for which the word is posited. So we say: The name may be other than the named, for the word 'wall' is other than the reality of the wall, and it may be identical to it, for the word 'name' is for the word that indicates a meaning abstracted from time, and among words is the word 'name', so the word 'name' becomes a name for itself, and here the name and the named are unified. He said: This is what I have on this issue.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey of the different scholarly positions on the relationship between the 'name' (ism), the 'named' (musammā), and the 'act of naming' (tasmīya). The author begins by citing al-Amidi that rational consensus affirms a distinction between tasmiya (the act of naming) and musammā (the named thing). He then presents the main view of the Ash'ari school that tasmiya is the actual utterances that indicate, while ism is the meaning indicated. Within the school, Ibn Furak holds that every name is identical to its named: for example, saying 'Allah' indicates a name that is the named itself. Others distinguish: some names are identical (like 'existent' and 'essence'), some are other (like 'creator', where the named is the essence and the name is the act of creation), and some are neither identical nor other (like 'knower', where the named is the essence and the name is knowledge, which is neither identical nor other). The Mu'tazila equate ism with tasmiya. Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub argues that 'ism' is equivocal and understood by context. The commentary then elaborates on Ibn Furak's view: he uses 'ism' for the literal signification of the word, and if 'musammā' means the thing for which the word is posited, identity holds; if it means the essence, then identity is understood as predicative union. Those who oppose him take 'musammā' as the essence and consider signification broader than literal. For attribute names like 'creator', they say the signification is the act of creation, which is other than the essence, following the principle that attributes of action are other than the subject. Al-Ash'ari himself held that the literal signification of an essential name is identical to the named, while for active attributes it is other, and for attributes like 'knower' it is neither. The author dismisses the need for extra explanation from the commentator of al-Maqasid. The discussion of the Quranic verses shows that two usages exist: one where 'name' means the named (as in 'Glorify the name of your Lord'), and one where it refers to the word itself. Al-Razi concludes that the dispute is largely verbal: 'ism' is the particular word, 'musammā' is the thing for which it is posited, and identity or difference depends on the case, with the word 'name' being a name for itself when considered as a word.

Al-Razi called this lengthy discussion 'superfluous' and refocused on a simple definition. The Four Divisions of Names and the Name from Essence
Classification of names into four types and their applicability to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of names (from essence, part, external attribute, or action) and the theological debate about whether a name from God's essence is possible.

Core Idea
Not all names of God derive from His essence; some stem from His actions or attributes.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces a foundational classification in Islamic theology: names applied to things fall into four types. After establishing this framework, the author examines which types can apply to Allah, with special attention to the problematic category of names taken from the essence—a category that hinges on whether Allah's essence can be intellectually grasped.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of names
Carefully read the opening lines that list the four divisions: from the essence, from a part, from an external attribute, or from an action. Notice that the author presents these as absolute divisions.
The four types are exhaustive for any name applied to a thing.
2
Understand each division
Pause to reflect on each type: a name from essence (e.g., 'Zayd' referring to his essence), from a part (e.g., 'hand' for a person), from an external attribute (e.g., 'wise' as a description), or from an action (e.g., 'creator').
Think of concrete examples from everyday language.
3
Focus on the name from essence for God
The passage then asks which of these apply to God and zeroes in on the name from essence. It explains that this depends on whether God's essence can be intellectually grasped (ta'aqqul). Read the arguments for and against.
This is a key point in Islamic theology: some schools deny that we can have a name representing God's essence directly.
4
Appreciate the nuance
The author adds a nuance: even if full grasp of the quiddity is impossible, one might still intellectually grasp 'some essence', which would allow a name. Also, later comments clarify that 'name' itself can be a name for a word, but that is a meta-discussion.
This shows the subtlety of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : في أقسام الاسم
اللفظ
( في أقسام الاسم، اعلم أن الاسم الذي يطلق على الشيء (إما أن يؤخذ من الذات ) بأن يكون المسمى به ذات الشيء من حيث هو ( أو من جزئها أو من وصفها الخارجي أو من الفعل ) الصادر عنه فهذه هي أقسام الاسم على الإطلاق ( ثم ننظر أيها الله يمكن في حق تعالى، أما المأخوذ من الذات ففرع تعقلها، وقد تكلمنا فيه) فمن ذهب إلى جواز تعقل ذاته جوز أن يكون له اسم بإزاء حقيقته المخصوصة، ومن ذهب إلى امتناع تعقلها لم يجوز له اسماً مأخوذاً من ذاته لأن وضع الاسم لمعنى فرع ووسيلة إلى تفهيمه فإذا لم يمكن أن يعقل ويفهم فلا يتصور بإزائه وفيه بحث لأن الخلاف في تعقل كنه ذاته ووضع الاسم لا يتوقف عليه إذ يجوز أن يعقل ذات ما
تعقله
قوله: (أي مسماه) لأن التسبيح والتعظيم إنما يتعلق بالمسمى لا باسمه وقديمنع ذلك بأنه كما يجب تنزيه ذاته وصفاته تعالى عن النقائض كذلك يجب تعظيم الألفاظ الموضوعة لها وتنزيهها عن الرفث وسوء الأدب، وقيل: الاسم في الآيتين مقحم وكذا في قوله : إلى الحول ثم اسم السلام عليكما .
قوله فيكون لفظ الاسم اسماً لنفسه لكن لا باعتبار خصوصه لأن الوضع للكلي لا : ( للجزئيات بل باعتبار أنه اسم كما حقق في المعاني والمغايرة بالكلية والجزئية لا تضر العينية في هذا البحث ما لم يتضمن معنى زائداً كالخلق والعلم
قوله : ( إذ يجوز أن يعقل إلخ) وإنما لم يجب بجواز أن يكون الواضع هو الله تعالى كما هو
The Second Purpose: On the Divisions of the Name. The word. (On the divisions of the name. Know that the name applied to a thing is either taken from the essence by making the named the thing's essence as such, or from its part, or from its external attribute, or from the action emanating from it. These are the absolute divisions of the name. Then we consider which of them can apply to the Exalted God. As for what is taken from the essence, it is a branch of intellectual grasp of it, and we have discussed it. So whoever holds that it is possible to intellectually grasp His essence allows that He has a name corresponding to His particular reality. And whoever holds that it is impossible to intellectually grasp it does not allow a name taken from His essence, because positing a name for a meaning is a branch and a means to making it understood. If it cannot be intellectually grasped and understood, then a name corresponding to it is inconceivable. There is a discussion in this, because the disagreement about intellectually grasping the quiddity of His essence and the positing of a name does not depend on it, since it is possible to intellectually grasp some essence. His saying: (i.e., His named) because glorification and magnification pertain to the named, not to His name. But it is objected that just as it is obligatory to declare His essence and attributes transcendent above imperfections, so too it is obligatory to magnify the words set for them and declare them transcendent above obscenity and bad manners. And it is said that the name in the two verses is redundant, and likewise in his saying: 'until the year, then the name of peace be upon you both.' His saying: thus the word 'name' becomes a name for itself, but not by virtue of its particularity, because the positing is for the universal, not for particulars, but by virtue of its being a name as established in semantics. And the difference in universality and particularity does not harm identity in this discussion as long as it does not imply an additional meaning like creation and knowledge. His saying: (since it is possible to intellectually grasp etc.) And he did not respond by saying that the positor could be God, exalted, as He is.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the divisions of names (aqsām al-ism) and a discussion of their applicability to God. The author states that a name applied to a thing is taken in one of four ways: (1) from the essence itself, meaning the named is the thing's essence as such; (2) from a part of the essence; (3) from an external attribute; (4) from an action emanating from the thing. After listing these, he asks which can apply to God. The name taken from the essence depends on whether God's essence can be intellectually grasped (ta'aqqul). If one says it is possible (as the Ash'aris generally hold), then a name corresponding to His particular reality is allowed. If one says it is impossible (as some maintain), then no name can be taken from His essence, because a name is a means to understanding, and if the essence cannot be understood, naming it is inconceivable. However, the author notes a nuance: the dispute about grasping the quiddity of God's essence does not necessarily prevent naming, because it is possible to grasp some essence even if not fully. The commentary then addresses a previous point: the Quranic verses 'Glorify the name of your Lord' are interpreted by some as meaning 'Glorify your Lord' (i.e., the named), but the objection is that the words themselves also deserve respect. Another view says 'name' in such verses is redundant (muqham). The commentary on the word 'name' being a name for itself clarifies that this is by virtue of its being a name universally, not its particular form. The discussion concludes that identity in this context is not harmed by universality versus particularity unless additional meaning (like creation or knowledge) is involved. The final comment notes that the objector did not consider that the positor could be God Himself.

اسم من الجزء
اسم من الوصف الخارجي
اسم من الفعل
تعقل الذات
وضع الاسم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All names of God are taken from His essence, so we can directly name His essence.
Correction: The passage shows that names can be taken from actions or attributes as well, and a name from essence is only possible if His essence can be intellectually grasped—a contested point.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Names applied to things fall into four categories, and the name taken from God's essence depends on whether His essence can be intellectually grasped.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High,' do you pause to consider whether you are only uttering a word, or are you truly magnifying the One whose essence you cannot fully comprehend? How does this awareness change your recitation today?
?
The scholars debated whether God can have a name taken from His essence because it requires intellectual grasp. You claim to know God, but in your intimate moments of worship, do you ever acknowledge that His reality remains beyond your understanding, or do you act as if you have Him figured out?
?
Every time you call upon Allah, you are using a name. But on what basis? Is it because you believe you can grasp His essence, or because you trust in His actions and attributes? This lesson forces you to examine the foundation of your invocation. Will you now reflect on which category your understanding of His names falls into?

The Name and the Named: A Linguistic Debate
al-Jurjani presents the dispute over name-named identity and declares it linguistically trivial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dispute over whether the name (ism) is identical to the named (musanna) and why al-Jurjani considers it a trivial linguistic issue.

Core Idea
The debate over name vs. named is linguistic, not theological.

This lesson examines a debate among theologians about the relationship between the name and the named. Al-Jurjani presents different positions—from the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and al-Ghazali—and then dismisses the entire discussion as superfluous. You'll see how he distinguishes between the word and its referent, and why he thinks lengthy theological treatment is unwarranted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow: first the author's own note on usage, then al-Razi's report, then the resolution.
Pay attention to the shift from author's voice to al-Razi's quotation.
2
Identify the three positions
In al-Razi's summary, find the Ash'ari, Mu'tazili, and Ghazali views. Note how each relates 'name' to 'named' or 'naming'.
The Mu'tazili view is 'tasmīya' (act of naming), not the word itself.
3
Understand al-Razi's resolution
Al-Razi defines 'name' as the specific word and 'named' as the meaning. He gives two examples: 'wall' (different) and 'name' (identical). Reflect on why this resolves the debate.
The debate collapses because the relationship depends on the specific case.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السابع - المقصد الثاني : في أقسام الاسم الله فيكون عين المسمى وكلا الاستعمالين ثابت كما في قولك الأسماء والأفعال والحروف، وقوله تعالى : سبح اسم ربك الأعلى ﴾ [الأعلى : ١] وتبارك اسم ربك [الرحمن : ۷۸]، أي مسماه. وقول لبيد : ثم اسم السلام عليكما. لكن هذا بحث لغوي لا فائدة فيه هاهنا . وقال الإمام الرازي: المشهور عن أصحابنا أن الاسم هو اسم المسمى وعن المعتزلة أنه التسمية وعن ) الغزالي أنه مغاير لهما لأن النسبة وطرفيها متغايرة قطعا والناس قد طولوا في هذه المسألة وهو عندي فضول لأن الاسم هو المخصوص والمسمى ما وضع ذلك اللفظ بإزائه فنقول : الاسم قد يكون غير المسمى، فإن لفظة الجدار مغايرة لحقيقة الجدار وقد يكون عينه، فإن لفظ اسم للفظ الدال على المعنى المجرد عن الزمان، ومن جملة الألفاظ لفظ الاسم فيكون لفظ الاسم اسماً لنفسه، فاتحد هاهنا الاسم أو المسمى، قال : فهذا ما عندي في هذه المسألة
The seventh observatory - the second purpose: on the divisions of the name 'Allah'. So it is identical to the named, and both usages are established, as in your saying 'nouns, verbs, and particles', and His saying 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' [Al-A'la 1] and 'Blessed is the name of your Lord' [Ar-Rahman 78], meaning His named. And the saying of Labid: 'then the name of peace be upon you both'. But this is a linguistic discussion with no benefit here. And Imam al-Razi said: The well-known from our companions is that the name is the name of the named, and from the Mu'tazila that it is the act of naming (tasmīya), and from al-Ghazali that it is different from both because the relation and its two ends are necessarily distinct. And people have gone on at length in this issue, and in my view it is superfluous because the name is the designated thing (al-makhṣūṣ) and the named is that for which the word is placed. So we say: the name may be other than the named, for the word 'wall' is different from the reality of the wall, and it may be identical to it, for the word 'name' is a word indicating a meaning stripped of time, and among the words is the word 'name', so the word 'name' is a name for itself, so here the name or the named become identical. He said: this is what I have on this issue.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among theologians about whether the name (ism) is identical to the named (musamma) or distinct from it. The author first notes that both usages exist in Arabic: sometimes 'name' refers to the named object, as in Quranic verses where glorifying the name means glorifying the named entity itself. He dismisses this as a purely linguistic discussion with no theological benefit. He then summarizes al-Razi's view: the Ash'arī school says the name is the name of the named (i.e., the word itself), the Mu'tazila say it is the act of naming (tasmīya), and al-Ghazali says it is different from both since a relation is distinct from its terms. Al-Razi finds the whole debate superfluous because the name is simply the particular word, and the named is the meaning for which the word is coined. He gives examples: the word 'wall' is not the same as the actual wall, but the word 'name' is a name for itself, causing identity in that specific case. The author concludes that the issue is trivial and closes.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
التسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate over whether the name is the same as the named has significant theological consequences.
Correction: Al-Jurjani dismisses it as a purely linguistic discussion with no benefit in theology.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's summary, what is the Mu'tazili position on the name?
A) The name is identical to the named.
B) The name is the act of naming (tasmīya).
C) The name is different from both the named and the act of naming.
D) The name is the word itself without relation.

Key Takeaway
The debate over name and named is a linguistic issue; the name is the word, the named is the meaning, and they can be distinct or identical depending on the example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever debated a fine point of theology that, after reading this lesson, you realize might be 'linguistic speculation with no benefit'? What does that reveal about your priorities in seeking knowledge?
?
When you recite 'Bismillah' or say the name 'Allah', do your heart and mind focus on the reality of God, or are you merely uttering a word? How would your worship change if you truly acted on the insight that the name indicates the named?
?
Al-Razi and al-Jurjani dismiss this debate as superfluous. Is there a theological or practical question you are currently struggling with that might also be a 'linguistic' distraction? How can you distinguish between essential matters and verbal disputes in your own study?

Linguistic and Technical Meanings of 'Nabi'
Lists three linguistic derivations and gives the technical definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three linguistic origins of the word 'Nabi' and the technical definition used by Sunni theologians.

Core Idea
The word 'Nabi' has deep linguistic roots pointing to announcement, elevation, or path.

Before discussing prophethood, it is essential to clarify the term itself. This lesson examines the linguistic roots of 'Nabi' – whether it comes from 'naba'' (news), 'nubuwwa' (height), or 'nabi' (path) – and how Sunni scholars define it technically as one to whom Allah says 'I have sent you.'

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage with translation
Read the passage once to get a sense of the author's presentation of different linguistic derivations and the technical definition.
2
Identify the three linguistic derivations
Find each proposed etymology: from 'naba'' (news), 'nubuwwa' (height), and 'nabi' (path). Note the reasoning behind each.
The passage uses 'qīla' (it is said) to indicate scholarly opinions.
3
Understand the technical definition
The People of Truth define a prophet as one to whom Allah says 'I have sent you.' This is a technical, conventional meaning.
This definition sets the stage for the discussion on circularity in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the linguistic roots matter—they highlight different aspects of prophethood: communication, high status, and being a guide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول في النبوات ] في النبوات، وفيه مقاصد تسعة ) . [ المقصد الأول : في معنى النبي ] العقائد الله ( في معنى النبي، وهو لفظ منقول في العرف عن مسماه اللغوي) إلى معنى عرفي، أما المعنى اللغوي ( فقيل : هو المنبئ ) واشتقاقه ) من النبأ ) فهو حينئذ مهموز لكنه يخفف ويدعم وهذا المعنى حاصل لمن اشتهر بهذا الاسم ( لإنبائه عن وقيل : ( النبي مشتق ( من النبوة وهو الارتفاع يقال : تنبأ فلان إذا ارتفع وعلا والرسول الله موصوف بذلك ( لعلو شأنه ( وسطوع برهانه ( وقيل : من النبي وهو الطريق لأنه وسيلة إلى الله تعالى (وأما مسماه ( في العرف فهو عند أهل الحق ) من الأشاعرة وغيرهم من المليين (من قال له الله تعالى ممن اصطفاه من عباده : ( أرسلتك ) إلى قوم قوله : ( يتوقف عليها السمع أي توقفاً قريباً فيخرج مباحث الإلهيات . قوله : (أو تتوقف هي على السمع أي بالاتفاق فيخرج مثل السمع والبصر والكلام فإنها عقلية عند البعض. مع مع حدوث قوله : ( فهو عند أهل الحق من قال الله تعالى إلخ ) قال الأبهري : النبوة عبارة عن هذا القول كونه متعلقا بالمخاطب ولما كان التعلق والمتعلق به غير قديم لا يلزم قدم النبوة وإن كان قول الله تعالى قديماً لأنها ليست عبارة عن مجرد ذلك القول بل عنه وعن تعلقه بالمتعلق الحادث وأنت خبير بأن تفسير النبي بما ذكر ينبغي أن يبنى على حدوث الألفاظ إذ قدمها التعلق أو قدمه أيضاً مع حدوث المتعلق غير معقول فإن قلت : ذلك التفسير يفضي إلى توقف النبوة على ثبوت الكلام وقد استدل عليه فيما سبق بما تواتر من الأنبياء وهل هذا إلا دور صريح، قلت : الذي توقف عليه النبوة هو الكلام اللفظي وهو من قبيل إيجاد الأصوات والحروف الغير المتوقف على الكلام النفسي القائم به تعالى والمأخوذ من الشرع هو هذا فلا دور .
The First Observatory: On Prophethoods, and it contains nine aims. [The First Aim: On the Meaning of Prophet.] (On the meaning of Prophet, and it is a term transferred in customary usage from its linguistic meaning) to a conventional meaning. As for the linguistic meaning, it is said: it is al-munbi' (the one who gives news), and its derivation) from al-naba' (news). So it is then hamzated, but it is lightened and supported. This meaning holds for one who is famous by this name because of his giving news. And it is said: (the Prophet is derived from al-nubuwwa (height), and it is elevation. It is said: tanabba'a fulan if he rose and became high. And the Messenger of Allah is described with that) because of the loftiness of his status) and the brilliance of his proof. And it is said: from al-nabi (the path), because he is a means to Allah. (As for its meaning) in custom, it is according to the People of Truth) from the Ash'aris and others among the milla-affiliated (that one to whom Allah, may He be exalted, said, from among those He chose from His servants: 'I have sent you' to a people. His saying: 'upon which hearing depends, i.e., proximate dependence, so the discussions of divine matters are excluded.' His saying: 'or they depend upon hearing, i.e., by consensus, so such as hearing, sight, and speech are excluded, for they are rational according to some.' With the occurrence of His saying: 'He is, according to the People of Truth, one to whom Allah said, etc.' al-Abhari said: Prophethood is an expression of this speech, being related to the addressee. And since the relation and the thing related are not eternal, the eternality of prophethood does not follow, even if the speech of Allah is eternal, because it is not an expression of mere speech, but of it and its relation to the temporal related. And you are aware that the interpretation of Prophet as mentioned should be based on the temporality of words, because if the relation is eternal, or it is also eternal with the temporal related, it is inconceivable. For if you say: 'That interpretation leads to the dependence of prophethood on the establishment of speech, and it was argued for it earlier by what has been transmitted from the prophets. Is this not clear circularity?' I say: 'What prophethood depends on is verbal speech, which is of the kind of creating sounds and letters, which does not depend on the internal speech subsisting in Him, may He be exalted, and what is taken from the Law is this, so there is no circularity.'
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a combined main text and commentary covering the definition of 'Nabi'. The main text first discusses the linguistic meanings: it can mean 'one who gives news' (from naba'), 'one who is elevated' (from nubuwwa), or 'path' (from nabi). Then the technical meaning according to Ahl al-Haq (Ash'aris and other Muslims) is given: one to whom Allah says 'I have sent you'. The commentary by al-Jurjani then clarifies the definition. He quotes al-Abhari who explains that prophethood is an expression of the divine speech (the saying 'I have sent you') as it relates to the recipient. Since the relation and the recipient are temporal, prophethood is temporal, even though Allah's speech attribute is eternal. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if prophethood depends on the existence of divine speech, and that speech was proven by the miracles of prophets, isn't that circular? The answer distinguishes verbal speech (created sounds and letters) from the internal, eternal speech attribute. Prophethood depends on the temporal verbal speech, not the eternal attribute, so no circularity occurs. The commentator emphasizes that the legal definition of prophethood is based on the temporal verbal command.

Key Terms
النبي
النبوة
النبأ
العرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'Nabi' has only one clear linguistic origin.
Correction: Scholars have proposed three different roots: naba' (news), nubuwwa (height), and nabi (path), each highlighting a different aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a proposed linguistic derivation for 'Nabi'?
A) From naba' (news)
B) From nubuwwa (height)
C) From nabi (path)
D) From nabiyya (prophethood)

Key Takeaway
The word 'Nabi' has multiple linguistic roots, but technically it refers to one whom Allah specifically addresses with a mission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophet is called 'Nabi' because he is a path to Allah. Are you actually using the Prophet ﷺ as your path to Allah in your daily struggles, or are you relying on your own schemes? What concrete step will you take today to follow his path more closely?
?
If the prophet's elevation comes from Allah's choice, what does that say about your own 'elevation' in life? Do you seek status through worldly means or through being a means for others to reach Allah? Honestly, which pursuit gets more of your energy?
?
The technical definition says a prophet is one to whom Allah says 'I have sent you.' Have you ever internalized that the Prophet's authority is directly from Allah? When you hesitate to obey a command from the Sunnah, are you not effectively doubting that Allah sent him? What will you change about your obedience today?

Resolving Circularity in Defining Prophethood
Presents and resolves the circularity objection using the distinction between verbal and internal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentary resolves the apparent circularity in defining prophethood by distinguishing between eternal internal speech and temporal verbal speech.

Core Idea
Prophethood depends on temporal verbal speech, not eternal divine speech, so no circularity occurs.

The technical definition of prophethood as 'Allah's saying 'I have sent you'' raises a problem: if prophethood is defined by divine speech, and divine speech is proven by prophets, then it seems circular. This lesson explains the commentator's crucial distinction between internal speech (kalām nafsī) and verbal speech (kalām lafẓī).

Guided Path
1
Identify the circularity objection
Read the objection: the definition of prophethood depends on divine speech, but divine speech was proven by prophets. Is this not circular?
The objection is presented as 'fa-in qulta' in the text.
2
Understand al-Abhari's analysis
Al-Abhari points out that prophethood is an expression of divine speech as it relates to a temporal addressee. This relation is temporal, so prophethood is temporal.
Prophethood is not eternal even if Allah's speech attribute is eternal.
3
Grasp the distinction between two types of speech
The answer distinguishes verbal speech (kalām lafẓī) – created sounds and letters – from internal speech (kalām nafsī) – the eternal attribute of speech in Allah.
Prophethood depends on the temporal verbal speech, not the eternal attribute.
4
See the resolution
Since the prophethood-defining speech is the temporal verbal command, which is created, there is no circularity. The eternal speech is proven by other means.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Linguistic and Conventional Meaning of Nabi
Explanation of the linguistic derivations of nabi and its conventional definition by Ahl al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origins of the term 'nabi' and its precise conventional definition according to Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'aris and others), including the rejection of philosophical conditions.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a pure divine gift, not earned by human effort.

This lesson explores the term 'nabi' (prophet) by examining its linguistic roots and then establishing its technical meaning in Islamic theology. The passage begins by noting that the word is a conventional transfer from a linguistic sense to a specific religious sense. Three possible linguistic derivations are presented, after which the definition according to Ahl al-Haqq is given—a definition that emphatically excludes any conditions of acquired spiritual states or natural dispositions, affirming that prophethood is solely a mercy and gift from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once with the translation, noting the flow from linguistic to conventional meaning.
2
Identify linguistic derivations
Find the three proposed linguistic origins of 'nabi' and understand each one's significance.
The derivations are from 'news', 'elevation', and 'path'.
3
Understand conventional definition
Focus on the definition given by Ahl al-Haqq and why they reject conditions like ascetic practices or innate purity.
Prophethood is purely by divine choice, not human achievement.

المقصد الأول : في معنى النبي، وهو لفظ منقول في العرف عن مسماه اللغوي إلى معنى عرفي، أما المعنى اللغوي فقيل : هو المنبئ واشتقاقه من النبأ فهو حينئذ مهموز لكنه يخفف ويدعم وهذا المعنى حاصل لمن اشتهر بهذا الاسم لإنبائه عن الله تعالى. وقيل : النبي مشتق من النبوة وهو الارتفاع يقال : تنبأ فلان إذا ارتفع وعلا والرسول الله موصوف بذلك لعلو شأنه وسطوع برهانه. وقيل : من النبي وهو الطريق لأنه وسيلة إلى الله تعالى. وأما مسماه في العرف فهو عند أهل الحق من الأشاعرة وغيرهم من المليين من قال له الله تعالى ممن اصطفاه من عباده : أرسلتك إلى قوم كذا وإلى الناس جميعاً أو بلغهم عني ونحوه من الألفاظ المفيدة لهذا المعنى كبعثتك ونبئهم. ولا يشترط فيه أي في الإرسال شرط من الأعراض والأحوال المكتسبة بالرياضات والمجاهدات في الخلوات والانقطاعات. والاستعداد ذاتي صفاء الجوهر وذكاء الفطرة كما يزعمه الحكماء بل الله سبحانه وتعالى يختص برحمته من يشاء من عباده فالنبوة رحمة وموهبة متعلقة بمشيئه فقط وهو أعلم حيث يجعل رسالاته وفي دلالة هذه الآية على المطلوب نوع خفاء كما لا يخفى. وهذا الذي ذهب إليه أهل الحق بناء على القول بالقادر المختار الذي يفعل ما يشاء ويختار ما يريد.
The first aim: on the meaning of 'nabi'. It is a term transferred in convention from its linguistic meaning to a conventional meaning. As for the linguistic meaning, it is said: it is 'al-munbi' (the one who gives news), and its derivation is from 'al-naba'' (news); it is then hamzated, but it is lightened and supported. This meaning is attained by one who is famous by this name because of his giving news from God. And it is said: 'nabi' is derived from 'al-nubuwwah' (elevation), which is height; it is said: so-and-so 'tanabba'a' if he became elevated and high, and the Messenger of God is described thus due to the loftiness of his status and the clarity of his proof. And it is said: from 'al-nabi' (the path) because he is a means to God. As for its meaning in convention, according to Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) from the Ash'aris and others among the milla-followers, it is: one to whom God, from among His chosen servants, says: 'I have sent you' to such-and-such people and to all people, or 'convey from Me' and similar expressions conveying this meaning like 'I have commissioned you' and 'give them news'. No condition is stipulated for it (i.e., for the sending) of accidental attributes or states acquired by spiritual exercises and struggles in seclusions and retreats. Nor is [it conditioned by] a natural disposition, purity of substance, and sharpness of innate disposition as the philosophers claim. Rather, God the Exalted and High singles out with His mercy whom He wills among His servants. Prophethood is a mercy and a gift dependent solely on His will. And He is most knowing where to place His message, and there is a kind of subtlety in the indication of this verse to the desired point, as is not hidden. And this is what Ahl al-Haqq have held, based on the doctrine of the powerful free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) who does what He wills and chooses what He desires.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'nabi' (prophet) by first examining its linguistic origins and then establishing its conventional meaning according to Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'aris and others). It begins by stating that the word is a term transferred in convention from its linguistic sense to a technical sense. Three linguistic derivations are given: (1) from 'al-naba'' (news), meaning the one who gives news from God; (2) from 'al-nubuwwah' (elevation), indicating high status; (3) from 'al-nabi' (path), as he is a means to God. The conventional definition is then given: a person to whom God says 'I have sent you' or 'convey from Me', etc. This definition explicitly rejects any conditions of acquired states or natural disposition (as claimed by philosophers). Prophethood is purely a divine gift, dependent only on God's will. The passage references the verse 'He is most knowing where to place His message' (Quran 6:124) and notes that its indication is subtle. It concludes that this position is based on the Ash'ari doctrine of the free agent (qādir mukhtār).

النبوة
النبأ
أهل الحق
القادر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood can be achieved through personal effort, ascetic practices, or by possessing a natural disposition of purity and intelligence.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Haqq, prophethood is solely a divine gift (rahman and mawhiba) dependent only on God's will, not on any human acquisition or innate qualities.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the conventional definition of 'nabi'?
A) One who has attained high spiritual rank through ascetic practices
B) One to whom God says 'I have sent you' or 'convey from Me'
C) One who has innate purity and sharp intellect
D) One who can perform miracles

Key Takeaway
The conventional definition of 'nabi' according to Ahl al-Haqq is one whom God directly appoints through a divine address, without any conditions of human effort or natural disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The philosophers claim that prophethood requires a special natural disposition. Is there any area of your religious life where you think you are naturally 'better' or more worthy of God's favor? Will you now consciously reject that pride and attribute everything to His mercy?
?
If prophethood is a mercy purely based on God's will, what does that mean for how you view the prophets and their messages? Does this humble you, knowing that you too are entirely dependent on God's choice for any guidance? What one concrete step will you take today to rely more on His will and less on your own striving?

Definition of Miracle and Its Threefold Investigation
Al-Jurjani defines miracle and introduces the threefold investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of a miracle in kalam and the three aspects scholars investigate.

Core Idea
A miracle is an act intended to prove prophethood.

Al-Jurjani begins by defining the miracle in precise technical terms. Before listing its conditions, he outlines three angles scholars examine: the conditions themselves, how miracles occur, and how they point to truth. This lesson covers that framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise style.
The passage uses parentheses for explanatory inserts.
2
Identify the definition
Find the definition of miracle: 'that by which is intended the manifestation of the truthfulness of one who claims to be a messenger of Allah.' Understand the key element of intentionality.
3
Note the threefold investigation
The passage mentions three matters: conditions, occurrence, and indication. These structure the entire discussion on miracles.
This sets the agenda for the next sections.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why is it important that a miracle is defined by its purpose (to attest to truth) rather than just being extraordinary?
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في حقيقة المعجزة وهي ( بحسب الاصطلاح ( عندنا ) عبارة عن (ما قصد به) إظهار صدق من ادعى أنه رسول الله والبحث فيها عن أمور ثلاثة : ( عن شرائطها، وكيفية حصولها ووجه (دلالتها على صدق مدعى الرسالة. (البحث الأول : في شرائطها وهي سبع : ( الشرط (الأول : أن يكون فعل الله أو ما يقوم مقامه) من التروك ، وإنما اشترط ذلك ) لأن التصديق منه ) أي من الله تعالى ( لا يحصل بما ليس من قبله وقولنا : أو ما يقوم مقامه ليتناول التعريف ( مثل ما إذا قال : معجزتي أن أضع يدي على رأسي وأنتم لا تقدرون عليه ) أي على وضع أيديكم على رؤوسكم ( ففعل وعجزوا فإنه معجز) دال على صدقه ( ولولا فعل لله ثمة فإن عدم خلق القدرة ) فيهم على ذلك الوضع (ليس فعلاً) صادر عنه تعالى بل هو عدم صرف ( ومن جعل الترك وجودياً ) بناء على أنه الكف ( حذفه ) لعدم الحاجة إليه فالشرط عنده كون المعجزة من فعل الله، وفي كلام الآمدي أن المعجز إن كان عدمياً كما هو أصل شيخنا، فالمعجز عدم خلق القدرة فلا يكون فعلاً، وإن كان وجودياً كما ذهب إليه بعض أصحابنا فالمعجز هو خلق العجز فيهم فيكون فعلاً فلا حاجة إلى قولنا: أو ما يقوم مقامه .
Concerning the reality of the miracle: it is, according to technical terminology in our school, an expression for that by which is intended the manifestation of the truthfulness of one who claims to be a messenger of Allah. And the investigation concerning it involves three matters: its conditions, the manner of its occurrence, and the way it indicates the truthfulness of the claimant of messengership. The first inquiry: concerning its conditions, which are seven. The first condition: that it be an act of Allah or what stands in its place, such as abstentions. It is stipulated because attestation from Him, i.e., from Allah Most High, cannot be obtained by something not from Him. Our statement 'or what stands in its place' is to include in the definition the case such as when he says: 'My miracle is that I place my hand on my head and you are not able to do it,' i.e., to place your hands on your heads. Then he does it and they are unable—it is a miracle indicating his truthfulness. And if there were no act of Allah there, then the non-creation of ability in them for that placing is not an act proceeding from Him, but rather a pure non-existence. And whoever considers abstention as existent, based on it being a refraining, drops it because there is no need for it. So the condition according to him is that the miracle be from an act of Allah. And in the words of al-Amidi: if the miracle is non-existent, as is the principle of our teacher, then the miracle is the non-creation of ability and thus not an act; but if it is existent, as some of our companions hold, then the miracle is the creation of incapacity in them and thus an act, so there is no need for our statement 'or what stands in its place.'
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines the miracle and begins enumerating its conditions. It first gives the definition: a miracle is an act intended to manifest the truth of a claimant to prophethood. It then states that the investigation of miracles involves three matters: their conditions, how they occur, and how they indicate truth. The first condition is that the miracle must be an act of Allah or something that takes its place, like abstentions (e.g., preventing people from doing something). The reason is that divine attestation cannot come from anything other than Allah. The phrase 'or what stands in its place' is added to include cases like when a prophet says his miracle is that he places his hand on his head and others cannot do it—the miracle is not his action but the inability of others, which is not a positive act but a privation. The author then discusses a disagreement: if one considers abstention (tark) as an existent entity (based on it being a refraining), then the condition that the miracle be an act of Allah suffices, and there is no need for 'or what stands in its place.' Al-Amidi is cited: if the miracle is non-existent (the non-creation of ability), it is not an act; if it is existent (creation of incapacity), it is an act, so again the additional phrase is unnecessary.

Key Terms
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of a miracle according to Al-Jurjani's definition?
A) To display supernatural power.
B) To manifest the truthfulness of a prophet claimant.
C) To create awe in people.
D) To prove the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is defined by its purpose: to attest to a prophet's truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle, do you immediately see it as a personal sign from Allah calling you to faith, or do you treat it as just a historical curiosity? What would change if you truly lived as if miracles are real and intentional?
?
The definition says a miracle is 'intended to manifest truthfulness'. Has your own life ever been pierced by a moment that felt like a divine sign—something that broke the normal pattern? If so, how did you respond? If not, are you open to the possibility that Allah might send you such a sign?
?
You claim to believe in the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). On what basis? Is it because of the miracle of the Qur'an? If that miracle were not accompanied by the claim of prophethood, would it still be a miracle? Examine your certainty: is it built on the purpose of the miracle or just the wonder of it?

First Condition: The Miracle Must Be from Allah
Al-Jurjani details the first condition and the debate over non-existent miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first condition of a miracle—that it must be an act of Allah or its equivalent—and the debate over non-existent miracles.

Core Idea
A miracle must be from Allah; even an abstention can serve as a miracle.

The first condition of a miracle is that it must be a divine act or something that takes its place. Al-Jurjani explains why this is necessary and clarifies the subtle case where the miracle is not a positive act but a privation, such as the inability of others to imitate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first condition
This passage is longer and more detailed. Read it slowly, then the translation.
Pay attention to the parentheses and examples.
2
Understand why the miracle must be from Allah
The reason given: divine attestation cannot come from something not from Allah. This is a logical necessity.
3
See how the phrase 'or what stands in its place' covers abstentions
The example of placing the hand on the head shows that if others are unable to do it, that inability (a non-act) can be the miracle.
This expands the definition.
4
Examine the debate on existence and non-existence
Al-Jurjani discusses differing views: if abstention is considered an existent ('refraining'), then the extra phrase may be dropped. Al-Amidi's opinion is also cited.
This shows the nuance in kalam reasoning.

The First Condition of a Miracle: Act of God and Its Existential Debate
Defines the first condition and debates its existential status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate about whether the miracle must be an existential act of God or can be a non-existential absence, and the implications for defining a miracle.

Core Idea
A miracle's divine origin is undisputed, but its nature as an existent event is debated.

This passage from al-Āmidī delves into the first condition of a miracle: it must be an act of God. However, a key debate arises over whether the miracle is an existential thing (like creating inability) or a non-existential thing (like not creating ability). The author presents both views and their consequences for the definition of a miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two positions
Notice that the passage presents the debate: some say the miracle is non-existential (absence of ability), others say it is existential (creation of inability). Follow the argument for each.
2
Analyze the implications for the condition
Consider how each view affects the requirement that the miracle be an act of God. If it's non-existential, it's not a positive act; if existential, it is an act.
3
Reflect on the omission
The author mentions that some omit the phrase 'or what stands in its place' if the miracle is existential. Think about why this matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في حقيقة المعجزة وعجزوا فإنه معجز) دال على صدقه ( ولولا فعل لله ثمة فإن عدم خلق القدرة ) فيهم على ذلك الوضع (ليس فعلاً) صادر عنه تعالى بل هو عدم صرف ( ومن جعل الترك وجودياً ) بناء على أنه الكف ( حذفه ) لعدم الحاجة إليه فالشرط عنده كون المعجزة من فعل الله، وفي كلام الآمدي أن المعجز إن كان عدمياً كما هو أصل شيخنا، فالمعجز عدم خلق القدرة فلا يكون فعلاً، وإن كان وجودياً كما ذهب إليه بعض أصحابنا فالمعجز هو خلق العجز فيهم فيكون فعلاً فلا حاجة إلى قولنا: أو ما يقوم مقامه .
The first observatory - the second purpose: On the reality of the miracle. And they were incapacitated, for it is a miracle) indicating his truthfulness (And if not for an act of God there, then the non-creation of ability) in them for that situation (is not an act) emanating from Him, but rather a pure non-existence. (And whoever makes the non-action existential) based on it being the restraint (omitted it) due to no need for it, so the condition according to him is that the miracle is from the act of God. And in the speech of al-Amidi: If the miracle is non-existential, as is the principle of our teacher, then the miracle is the non-creation of ability, so it is not an act; and if it is existential, as some of our companions held, then the miracle is the creation of inability in them, so it is an act, and there is no need for our saying: 'or what stands in its place'.

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a heading and the first condition of a miracle. It begins by stating the topic: the reality of the miracle. The condition is that the miracle must be an act of God, but the author immediately presents a debate about its nature. The text first presents the view that if God does not create the ability (in the opponents), that non-creation is not an act but a pure non-existence. Then it mentions that some consider the 'non-action' (like refraining) as existential, but that is omitted. The core debate comes from al-Amidi: if the miracle is non-existential (like the absence of ability), it is not an act; if it is existential (like creating inability), then it is an act. The passage concludes that if it is existential, there is no need for the phrase 'or what stands in its place'.

The first condition of a miracle is that it must be from God, but theologians differ on whether the miracle itself is a positive existential event or a non-existential absence.

The Second Condition: Miracle Must Be Extraordinary
States the second condition and rebuts a related stipulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle must be extraordinary and how it differs from ordinary events.

Core Idea
A miracle must break the norm; otherwise it cannot prove prophethood.

Before we can recognize a true miracle, we need to know its defining features. The second condition states that a miracle must be extraordinary—something that cannot be matched by anyone, even a liar. This lesson explains the rationale and addresses a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get the flow. Notice the definition and the example of habitual events.
2
Identify the condition and its justification
The condition is that the miracle must be extraordinary. The justification is that ordinary events can be performed by anyone, including liars.
3
Consider the stipulation and rebuttal
Some scholars added that the miracle must not be within the prophet's power. The author rebuts this by arguing that the prophet's ability, when others lack it, is itself miraculous.
This rebuttal is key to the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الثاني : أن يكون ( المعجز) خارقاً للعادة إذ لا إعجاز دونه فإن المعجز ينزل من الله منزلة التصديق بالقول كما سيأتي وما لا يكون خارقا للعادة بل معتاداً كطلوع الشمس في كل يوم وبدو الأزهار في كل ربيع فإنه لا يدل على الصدق لمساواة غيره إياه في ذلك حتى الكذاب في دعوى النبوة وشرط (قوم) في المعجز ( أن لا يكون مقدوراً للنبي ) إذ لو كان مقدوراً له كصعوده إلى الهواء ومشيه على الماء لم يكن نازلاً منزلة التصديق من الله تعالى (وليس بشيء لأن قدرته مع عدم قدرة غيره عادة معجز) قال الآمدي : هل يتصور كون المعجزة مقدورة للرسول أم اختلفت الأئمة فيه فذهب بعضهم إلى أن المعجز فيما ذكر من المثال ليس هو الحركة بالصعود أو المشي لكونها مقدورة له بخلق الله فيه القدرة عليها إنما المعجز هناك هو نفس القدرة عليها وهذه القدرة ليست مقدورة له وذهب آخرون إلى أن نفس هذه الحركة معجزة من جهة كونها خارقة للعادة ومخلوقة لله تعالى وإن كانت مقدورة للنبي، وهو الأصح ، وإذا عرفت هذا فلا يخفى عليك ما في عبارة الكتاب من الاختلال قوله : ( إن كان عدمياً كما هو أصل شيخنا إلخ) هذا المنقول من الآمدي مخالف لما مر في المقصد الثامن من مقاصد القدرة فلينظر فيه . قوله : ( وشرط قوم إلخ) قيل : هذا مستدرك لأن معنى الشرط الأول أعني كون المعجزة فعل الله تعالى أن لا يكون للعبد دخل فيها بالكسب وجوابه أن معناه أن يكون مخلوقاً له تعالى يؤيده ما ذكره الشارح في شرح الكشاف أن شرط المعجزة أن يكون صادراً من الله تعالى، لأنها تصديق فعلي منه سبحانه وتعالى، وحينئذ يكون معنى قوله : وشرط قوم إلخ أنه شرط قدوم أن لا يكون مكسوباً للعبد أيضاً، ولا يقدح في هذا عدم قولنا بالقدرة المؤثرة للعبد أصلاً، كما لا يخفى ويؤيده أيضاً ما سيذكره في جواب أول شبه الطائفة الخامسة القائلة بأن المعجزة لا تدل على الصدق . قوله : ( كصعوده إلى الهواء إلخ) فإنه مقدور له بأقدار الله تعالى . قوله : ( وهو الأصح لأن المقصود تصديق النبي عليه السلام بتعجيز الغير وهو حاصل. قوله : (ما في عبارة الكتاب من الاختلال) لأن من شرط أن لا يكون المعجزة مقدورة للنبي قائل بأن نفس القدرة معجزة قيل : ويمكن حمل كلام المصنف على أن كون ذلك الفعل مقدوراً
The second condition: that the miracle be extraordinary, for there is no miraculousness without it. For the miracle descends from God as an affirmation by speech, as will come, and whatever is not extraordinary but habitual, like the rising of the sun every day and the appearance of flowers every spring, does not indicate truthfulness because others equal him in that, even the liar in his claim to prophethood. And some people stipulated for the miracle that it not be within the power of the prophet; for if it were within his power, like his ascending into the air and walking on water, it would not descend as an affirmation from God. (But it is nothing, because his power along with the inability of others customarily is miraculous.) Al-Amidi said: Is it conceivable that the miracle is within the power of the messenger? The imams differed: some held that the miracle in the mentioned example is not the motion of ascension or walking, because it is within his power by God's creation of ability in him; rather, the miracle is the ability itself, and this ability is not within his power. Others held that this motion itself is a miracle because it is extraordinary and created by God, even if it is within the prophet's power, and that is the correct view. When you understand this, what is problematic in the phrase of the book is not hidden from you. His saying: (If it is non-existential, as is the principle of our teacher, etc.) This transmitted from al-Amidi contradicts what passed in the eighth purpose of the purposes of power, so it should be examined. His saying: (And a group stipulated, etc.) It is said: This is redundant because the meaning of the first condition, namely that the miracle is the act of God, is that the servant has no involvement in it by acquisition. The answer is that its meaning is that it is created by Him, supported by what the commentator mentioned in the commentary of al-Kashshaf, that the condition of the miracle is that it emanates from God, because it is an actual affirmation from Him. Then the meaning of his saying 'and a group stipulated, etc.' is that it is a prior condition that it is not acquired by the servant either, and this is not harmed by our denial of effective power to the servant at all, as is not hidden. It is also supported by what he will mention in the answer to the first doubt of the fifth group who claim that miracles do not indicate truthfulness. His saying: (like his ascending into the air, etc.) for it is within his power by God's decrees. His saying: (and it is the most correct, because the purpose is to affirm the prophet, peace be upon him, by incapacitating others, and that is attained. His saying: (what is problematic in the phrase of the book) because whoever stipulates that the miracle not be within the power of the prophet says that the power itself is a miracle. It is said: it is possible to interpret the author's words as that the act being within his power...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the second condition of a miracle (being extraordinary) along with extensive commentary. It first states the condition clearly: the miracle must break the norm, otherwise it is like natural phenomena that everyone can observe and thus cannot distinguish a true prophet from a liar. Then it mentions a further stipulation made by some: the miracle must not be within the prophet's own power. The text rebuts this by noting that the prophet's ability, when others cannot match it, is itself miraculous. Al-Amidi's opinion is then cited: there are two views—either the miracle is the ability itself (not the action) or the action itself is the miracle because it is extraordinary. The correct view is the latter. The rest of the passage contains editorial glosses on various phrases: the first gloss notes an inconsistency with earlier discussions; the second explains the stipulation about the prophet's power is not redundant but reinforces that the miracle is not acquired by the prophet; the third and fourth notes affirm that the action is within the prophet's power but remains a miracle. The final note is incomplete, suggesting that the act being within the prophet's power does not negate its miraculous nature.

Key Terms
خارق للعادة
معجزة
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the prophet can perform the miracle, it is not a miracle.
Correction: The correct view is that the prophet's ability is God-given and the act is miraculous because it is extraordinary and beyond the capacity of others, even if the prophet himself can do it.

Checkpoint
What is the main justification for the second condition?
A) To ensure the miracle is beyond human capability.
B) To distinguish the prophet from ordinary people.
C) Because ordinary events can be matched by liars.
D) Because the prophet must be powerless.

Key Takeaway
A miracle must be extraordinary to serve as a distinctive sign of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle of the Prophet ﷺ, do you subconsciously think he performed it by his own power? How does the author's rebuttal challenge that assumption and redirect your focus to God's role?
?
The lesson says that habitual events like sunrise do not prove truthfulness because even a liar can claim them. In your own search for truth, have you ever settled for 'habitual' evidence—things that are common to all beliefs—rather than seeking the extraordinary sign that only God can provide?
?
If you were to defend the prophethood of Muhammad ﷺ to someone who doubts, would you be able to explain why the miracle must be extraordinary? Can you articulate it from this passage in a way that leaves no room for equivocation? Reflect on your readiness.

Dispute: Can a Miracle Be Within the Prophet's Power?
Al-Amidi's discussion on the possibility of the miracle being within the prophet's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on whether a miracle can be within the prophet's power and the correct view according to al-Amidi.

Core Idea
The miracle itself is extraordinary, even if the prophet has the ability to perform it.

After establishing the second condition, scholars debated a subtle sub-point: does the miracle need to be beyond the prophet's own power? Al-Amidi records two positions and gives his verdict. This lesson explores that discussion and clarifies the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's discussion
Read the passage from 'قال الآمدي' to the end. Notice the two opinions and the author's preference.
2
Identify the two views
The first view says the miracle is the ability itself, not the action. The second says the action itself is the miracle because it is extraordinary.
3
Understand the correct view
The correct view is that the action itself is the miracle, because the purpose is to affirm the prophet by incapacitating others.
4
Reflect on the editorial notes
The passage includes remarks on consistency with earlier discussions and the redundancy of the stipulation. These are advanced points.
You may skip them initially and focus on the main debate.

Conditions of Miracles: Inimitability and Manifestation on the Claimant
Defines third and fourth conditions for a miracle and clarifies explicit challenge ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth conditions for a miracle: that it be inimitable and that it appear on the hand of the claimant, and that explicit challenge is not always required.

Core Idea
A miracle must be both inimitable and appear on the claimant's hand, with or without explicit challenge.

This passage continues the discussion of the conditions for a miracle in the context of prophethood. Having established the first two conditions (likely in prior lessons), the author now presents the third and fourth conditions. The third condition is that the miracle cannot be opposed—this is the essence of miraculousness. The fourth condition is that it must be performed by the claimant of prophethood, so that it clearly serves as divine affirmation. A key point of debate is whether an explicit challenge is required; the author clarifies that circumstantial evidence suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style and the flow from the third to the fourth condition.
The passage begins with the third condition (inimitability) and then introduces the fourth (manifestation on the claimant).
2
Identify the Two Conditions
Find the phrase for the third condition: 'يتعذر معارضته' (impossible to oppose). Find the fourth condition: 'أن يكون ظاهراً على يد مدعي النبوة' (that it be manifest on the hand of the claimant of prophethood). Reflect on why each condition is essential.
The third condition defines the essence of miraculousness: it is something that cannot be matched or opposed.
3
Understand the Ruling on Explicit Challenge
Read the discussion about explicit challenge. The author rejects the view that explicit challenge is required. Instead, circumstantial evidence (قرائن الأحوال) suffices. Consider the example given: telling the claimant 'If you are a prophet, show a miracle' and he does so.
This shows that the context can imply a challenge even if words are not spoken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتعذر معارضته فإن ذلك حقيقة الإعجاز . الرابع: أن يكون ظاهراً على يد مدعي النبوة ليعلم أنه تصديق له وهل يشترط التصريح بالتحدي وطلب المعارضة كما ذهب إليه بعضهم ( الحق أنه لا يشترط بل يكفي قرائن الأحوال مثل أن يقال له أي لمدعي النبوة، (إن كنت نبياً فأظهر معجزاً، ففعل بأن دعا الله فاظهره فيكون ظهوره دليلاً على صدقه ونازلاً منزلة التصريح بالتحدي
It is impossible to oppose it, for that is the reality of miraculousness. Fourth: that it be manifest on the hand of the claimant of prophethood, so that it be known that it is an affirmation for him. And is explicit challenge and request for opposition stipulated, as some held? The truth is that it is not stipulated; rather, circumstantial evidence suffices, such as it being said to him, i.e., to the claimant of prophethood, 'If you are a prophet, then show a miracle,' and he does it by supplicating God and it appears, so its appearance becomes evidence of his truthfulness and takes the place of explicit challenge.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents two conditions concisely. The third condition (the end of the previous incomplete sentence) is that the miracle must be such that it cannot be opposed; that is the essence of miraculousness. Then the fourth condition is that the miracle must appear on the hand of the person claiming prophethood, so that it is clear that it is divine affirmation for him. There is a subsidiary question: is an explicit verbal challenge (such as 'produce something like this') required? The answer is no; it is enough that the context implies a challenge, like telling the claimant to show a miracle and he does so. The miracle's appearance then functions as an implicit challenge.

Key Terms
إعجاز
معارضة
ظاهر على يد
تحدي
قرائن الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a miracle only counts if preceded by an explicit verbal challenge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that explicit challenge is not required; circumstantial evidence can take its place.

Checkpoint
What is required for a miracle to serve as divine affirmation according to the fourth condition?
A) An explicit verbal challenge is always necessary.
B) The miracle must be performed by someone other than the claimant.
C) The miracle must appear on the hand of the claimant, with or without explicit challenge.
D) The miracle must be accompanied by a request for opposition.

Key Takeaway
A miracle must be both inimitable and performed by the claimant, and an explicit challenge is not necessary if the context implies one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith are you demanding an explicit, undeniable sign from God before you fully obey, when His circumstantial evidence—through creation, scripture, or answered prayers—has already laid the foundation? What would it take for you to stop waiting and start acting?
?
The passage shows that a miracle must appear on the claimant's hand. If you claim to follow the Prophet, what evidence in your life—your character, your priorities, your reactions—affirms that his message is true? And if that evidence is missing, what change will you make today?

Fifth Condition: Miracle Must Match the Claim
States the fifth condition and gives a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a claimed miracle must exactly match the claim itself, and why a mismatch fails to verify prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle not matching the claim is like a wrong key to a lock.

The fifth condition is about correspondence between the miracle and the claim. A prophet's miracle is a divine endorsement of his specific message. If the miracle doesn't match what he claimed, it cannot serve as proof. Sanusi illustrates this with a simple example: a claimant who says his miracle is reviving the dead but instead splits a rope — that doesn't confirm his claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise wording and the example given.
This is a short but crucial condition.
2
Understand the condition
The condition states that the miracle must be 'in agreement with the claim'. Ask yourself: what does 'agreement' mean here?
The example clarifies: the performed act must be exactly what was claimed.
3
Reflect on the logic
Why does a different miracle not indicate truthfulness? Because it does not take the place of God's affirmation of the specific claim. The miracle is a direct verification; a mismatch breaks the connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن يكون موافقاً للدعوى فلو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي ميتاً، ففعل خارقا آخر كنتق الحبل مثلاً ( لم يدل على صدقه لعدم تنزله منزلة تصديق الله إياه .
Fifth: that it be in agreement with the claim. For if he says: 'My miracle is that I revive a dead person,' and he performs another extraordinary act, like splitting a rope for example, it does not indicate his truthfulness because it does not take the place of God's affirmation of him.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This is a short but crucial condition. The miracle must exactly match the claim the prophet makes. If he claims one specific kind of miracle (e.g., reviving the dead) but then performs a different extraordinary act (e.g., splitting a rope), that other act does not serve as divine affirmation of his claim. The reason given is that the miracle is supposed to be a direct verification of the claim; a mismatch breaks the connection.

Key Terms
موافق للدعوى
تصديق
خارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary act performed by a claimant to prophethood automatically verifies his claim.
Correction: The act must be exactly the miracle he claimed, otherwise it does not count as divine affirmation.

Checkpoint
Why does performing a different miracle than claimed not indicate truthfulness?
A) Because it is not extraordinary.
B) Because the claim is not verified by that act.
C) Because the prophet is not capable.
D) Because the people do not accept it.

Key Takeaway
A miracle must match the exact claim to be accepted as divine verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been convinced by a powerful sign or argument that did not actually match the claim being made? How does this condition call you to examine the alignment between what is claimed and what is shown in matters of faith?
?
The claimant's mismatch exposes a lack of integrity. In your own life, where do your actions fail to match your words about your faith? Will you now ask God to help you align your deeds with your testimony, so that your life becomes a true sign of your beliefs?
?
If God's affirmation comes only when the sign exactly matches the claim, are you willing to subject all spiritual claims—including those you accept about yourself—to this rigorous standard? How would that change your daily accountability?
