The Miracle Must Not Contradict the Claim
Explains the sixth condition with two contrasting examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth condition for miracles and why a miracle that itself testifies against the claimant cannot prove his truthfulness.

Core Idea
A miracle that contradicts the claim invalidates the proof of prophethood.

Sanusi now turns to the sixth condition: the miracle must not itself testify against the claimant. He illustrates this with two cases: a lizard that calls the claimant a liar, and a revived person who denies him. The distinction between the miracle itself and the free action of a person is crucial. This lesson unpacks these examples and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the condition and the lizard example
Start by reading the statement of the sixth condition, then the first example where a lizard speaks and calls the claimant a liar. Notice that the miracle itself becomes the testifier against him.
The lizard has no choice; its speech directly contradicts the claim.
2
Examine the revival case
Now read the second example: reviving a dead person who then denies the claimant. The text discusses why this does not invalidate the miracle, since the revival itself does not deny—only the person does, and he is a free agent.
The key is that the miracle is the act of reviving, not the speech of the revived.
3
Analyze the subcases: life vs. immediate death
The passage considers whether it matters if the revived person lives on and persists in denial, or dies immediately. It presents al-Qadi's view and then the correct view that there is no difference because choice exists in both.
This reinforces that the person's free choice is independent of the miracle.
4
Differentiate from the lizard case
Finally, contrast the revival case with the lizard: in the lizard, the extraordinary event itself is the denial, so it contradicts the claim. In revival, the miracle is the reviving, which does not deny.
The lizard has no choice; the revived person does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أن لا يكون ما دعاه وأظهره ) من المعجزة ( مكذباً له فلو قال : معجزتي أن ينطق هذا الضب، فقال: إنه كاذب، لم يعلم به صدقه بل ازداد اعتقاد كذبه لأن المكذب هو نفس الخارق ( نعم لو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي هذا الميت فأحياه فكذبه ففيه احتمال والصحيح أنه لا يخرج بذلك عن كونه معجزاً لأن المعجز إحياؤه ) وهو غير مكذب له إنما المكذب هو ذلك الشخص بكلامه ( وهو بعد ذلك ) الإحياء ) مختار في تصديقه وتكذيبه ولم يتعلق به دعوى ) فلا يقدح تكذيبه في دلالة الإحياء على صدقه ( وقيل : (هذا) الذي ذكرنا من عدم خروجه عن كونه معجزاً إنما هو (إذا عاش بعده ) أي بعد الإحياء (زماناً) واستمر على التكذيب . قال الآمدي : لا أعرف في هذه الصورة خلافاً بين الأصحاب ( ولو خر ميتا في الحال ) قال القاضي : ( بطل الإعجاز لأنه كأنه أحيي للتكذيب) فصار مثل تكذيب الضب . ( والحق أنه لا فرق بين استمرار الحياة مع التكذيب وبين عدمه ( لوجود الاختيار في (الصورتين بخلاف الضب ) والظاهر أنه لا يجب تعيين المعجز) بل يكفي أن يقول : أنا آتي بخارق من الخوارق ولا يقدر أحد على أن يأتي بواحد منها. وفي كلام الآمدي أن هذا متفق عليه قال فإذا كان المعجز معيناً فلا بد في معارضته المماثلة وإذا لم يكن معيناً فأكثر الأصحاب على أنه لا بد فيها من المماثلة وقال القاضي : لا حاجة إليها وهو الحق لظهور المخالفة فيما ادعاه .
Sixth: that what he claimed and manifested of the miracle does not testify against him. For if he says: 'My miracle is that this lizard speaks,' and it says: 'He is a liar,' his truthfulness is not known from it; rather, his lying is increased. Because the testifier is the extraordinary event itself. Yes, if he says: 'My miracle is that I revive this dead person,' and he revives him and he (the revived) denies him, then there is a possibility, and the correct view is that it does not remove it from being a miracle, because the miracle is his revivification, and it does not testify against him; rather, the testifier is that person by his speech, and he after revivification is free in affirming or denying him, and no claim is attached to him. So his denial does not impair the indication of revivification for his truthfulness. And it is said: this that we mentioned of its not ceasing to be a miracle is only if he lives after it, i.e., after revivification, for a time and persists in denial. Al-Amidi said: I do not know any disagreement among the companions in this case. And if he dies immediately, al-Qadi said: the miraculousness is invalidated because it is as if he was revived for denial, so it becomes like the denial of the lizard. And the truth is that there is no difference between the continuation of life with denial and its absence, because choice exists in both situations, unlike the lizard. And it is apparent that specifying the miracle is not required; rather, it is sufficient that he says: 'I bring an extraordinary thing from among extraordinary things, and no one is able to bring any one of them.' And in the speech of al-Amidi, this is agreed upon. He said: if the miracle is specified, then opposition must be by similitude; and if it is not specified, most companions hold that similitude is necessary, but al-Qadi said there is no need for it, and that is the truth because the discrepancy in what he claimed is apparent.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the sixth condition. It first states that the miracle itself must not testify against the claimant. Two examples illustrate this: (1) If the claimant says a lizard will speak and the lizard calls him a liar, that undermines his claim because the miracle itself becomes a testimony against him. (2) A more complex case: if the claimant revives a dead person, and the revived person then denies the claimant, the miracle is not invalidated because the revival itself does not deny; the denial comes from a free agent. There is a nuance: some say this holds only if the revived person lives on and persists in denial; if he dies immediately, some (like al-Qadi) say the miracle is void. However, the text declares the correct view that there is no difference between living and dying, because choice exists in both, unlike the lizard which has no choice. The passage then adds a related point: specifying the exact miracle is not required; it is enough for the claimant to assert he will bring an extraordinary act beyond anyone's power. There follows a discussion on whether opposition requires similitude: if the miracle is specified, similitude is necessary; if not, most say similitude is needed, but al-Qadi says it is not, and that is deemed correct because the discrepancy is clear.

مكذب
خارق
إحياء
تكذيب
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who revives a dead person and the revived denies him invalidates the miracle.
Correction: The miracle is the reviving, not the speech; the denial comes from a free agent and does not affect the miracle's validity.

Checkpoint
Why does the lizard's speech in the first example invalidate the miracle, while the revived person's denial in the second does not?
A) Because the lizard is an animal and cannot lie.
B) Because the lizard's speech is the miracle itself, whereas the revival is the miracle and the denial is a separate act.
C) Because the claimant asked the lizard to speak truthfully.
D) Because the lizard's speech is in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is not invalidated by the subsequent free speech of a person, only by its own content.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear sign of Allah, like the beauty of creation or a miraculous event, do you immediately accept it, or do you let your own subjective feelings and choices cloud your judgment? This lesson shows that a miracle remains valid even if someone denies it; will you commit to recognizing Allah's signs even when others reject them?
?
Think of a time when you witnessed something that clearly pointed to truth but you hesitated because of what others said. In light of this lesson, are you willing to separate the sign itself from the words of people? How can you train yourself to focus on the message of Allah rather than the reactions of creation?
?
If a person denies the truth after witnessing a miracle, does that make the miracle less of a miracle? According to the correct view, no. Does this challenge your assumption that truth must always be accepted? Will you accept that you are responsible for your own choice, and that no amount of external signs forces belief? Make a commitment today to respond to clear truth, regardless of how others around you respond.

Specifying the Miracle and the Need for Similitude
Discusses whether miracles must be specified and the requirement of similitude in opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a prophet need not specify his miracle in advance, and the debate on whether opposition to an unspecified miracle requires similitude.

Core Idea
A prophet may claim a general miracle; if specified, similitude is needed, but al-Qadi says it is not.

Sanusi adds a further point: the claimant does not have to specify exactly what miracle he will perform; it is enough to say he will bring an extraordinary act beyond anyone's power. Then he addresses a related debate: when opponents challenge a miracle, must they produce something similar? The answer depends on whether the miracle was specified, with al-Qadi's view given preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on specifying the miracle
Start by reading the claim that it is not necessary to specify the exact miracle. The claimant can simply say he will bring an extraordinary thing.
This expands the flexibility of the miracle claim.
2
Understand the agreement on this point
The text says al-Amidi reports consensus on this. Know that the specification is not required.
3
Study the debate on similitude for opposition
Now read the discussion: if the miracle is specified, opposition must match it; if unspecified, most scholars require similitude, but al-Qadi says no need.
The text declares al-Qadi's view as correct because the discrepancy is clear.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does al-Qadi say no similitude is needed? Because when the miracle is unspecified, any impossibility demonstrates the claimant's truth, so it is apparent.
This emphasizes that the miracle's purpose is to prove truth, not to be matched.

The Seventh Condition: Timing of the Miracle
States the seventh condition on the temporal relationship between miracle and claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh condition for miracles: the proper timing of the miracle relative to the claim of prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle must be timely linked to the claim—neither early nor mismatched.

Theologians set conditions to ensure the miracle directly supports the prophet's claim. The seventh condition focuses on timing: the miracle must not precede the claim, but may accompany it without discrepancy or come after it with specific detail. This lesson examines that condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the text is incomplete—it likely continues with a detail about the delayed miracle.
The passage is a fragment from a longer discussion.
2
Identify the three timing possibilities
The passage lists three temporal relationships: preceding, concomitant, and delayed. See which ones are allowed and which are not.
Preceding is forbidden; concomitant is allowed without discrepancy; delayed is allowed with a detail.
3
Consider why preceding is forbidden
Think about why a miracle before the claim would not serve as a proof. How would it be disconnected from the prophet's message?
This reinforces the link between miracle and prophethood.
4
Reflect on the missing detail
The passage says 'according to a detail' for the delayed miracle. Without the full text, consider what that detail might be. Check your assumptions.
This condition is part of a larger system of conditions for miracles.

Source Text

(السابع : أن لا يكون ) المعجز ) متقدماً على الدعوى مقارناً لها بلا اختلاف أو متأخراً عنها على تفصيل من
(Seventh: that the miracle not be) preceding the claim, or concomitant with it without discrepancy, or delayed after it, according to a detail from
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This is a brief statement of the seventh condition regarding timing. The miracle should not precede the claim of prophethood; it may be simultaneous with the claim without any discrepancy, or it may come after the claim according to a certain detail. The text is cut off, so further elaboration is missing. The condition ensures that the miracle is clearly connected to the claim.

Correction: The miracle must be directly connected to the claim; preceding miracles do not clearly support a future claim.

The Act and the Power
Defends the coherence of the condition on power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle can be within the prophet's power yet still be beyond human capacity, resolving an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
A miracle's impossibility lies in the power, not the act.

Sanusi defends a subtle condition: the act of a miracle is within the prophet's potential power, but the power to do it is not. This note clarifies that 'within power' does not mean 'possible for others.' Understanding this preserves the integrity of the definition of miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the phrase 'فلا خلل' which signals a defense against a potential objection.
The objection might be that if the act is within the prophet's power, it cannot be a miracle.
2
Identify the analogy
The text compares the act being مقدور (within power) to power itself being غير مقدور (not within power). Understand the parallel: just as the power to create is not created, the act's possibility does not negate the miracle.
Reflect on 'كنفس القدرة في عدم المقدورية'.
3
Apply to the condition
The condition of 'عدم المقدورية' (non-possibility for others) applies to the power, not the act. This note resolves the apparent tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من للنبى عليه السلام دون غيره معجزة، فلا خلل حينئذ في العبارة ولا يخفى بعد تسليم مساعدة عبارة المصنف، لذلك المعنى أن كون الفعل مقدوراً كنفس القدرة في عدم المقدورية وأنه لا معنى للرد لكونه معجزاً على من شرط عدم مقدورية المعجزة، فتأمل. قوله : (ظاهراً على يد مدعي النبوة) وقولهم كرامة الأولياء معجزة لنبيهم إنما هو بطريق التشبيه أي هي كالمعجزات في دلالتها على صدق نبيهم . قوله : ( وهو بعد ذلك مختاراً إلخ) هذا يدل على أنه لو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي هذا الضب الميت فأحياه فنطق أنه كاذب لا يعلم به صدقه تأمل.
From, for the prophet, peace be upon him, without others, a miracle, so there is no flaw in the phrase, and it is not hidden after accepting the assistance of the author's speech, therefore the meaning is that the act being within his power is like the power itself in non-possibility, and that there is no meaning to the refutation because it is a miracle against whoever stipulated the non-possibility of the miracle, so reflect. His saying: (manifest on the hand of the claimant of prophethood) and their saying that the wonders (karamat) of saints are a miracle for their prophet is only by way of analogy, i.e., they are like miracles in their indication of the truthfulness of their prophet. His saying: (and he after that is free, etc.) this indicates that if he said: 'My miracle is that I revive this dead lizard,' and he revived it and it spoke that he is a liar, his truthfulness is not known from it; reflect.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage consists of several editorial notes. The first note defends the coherence of the earlier discussion: the fact that the act is within the prophet's power does not detract from its miraculousness, because the power itself is not within his power. The analogy is drawn between the act and the power in terms of being beyond human capacity. The second note explains a common phrase: when saints perform wonders (karamat), they are called miracles of the prophet only by analogy, because they indicate the prophet's truthfulness. The third note revisits the sixth condition: if the claimant says his miracle is reviving a dead lizard and it speaks and calls him a liar, then his truthfulness is not known; the note suggests further reflection.

Key Terms
معجزة
مقدور
عدم المقدورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act is within a prophet's power, it cannot be a miracle because it is possible for him.
Correction: The act is within his power as a human, but the actual power to produce it is from Allah and beyond human capacity. The condition of impossibility applies to others, not to the prophet's own power.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the act being within the prophet's power?
A) It creates a flaw in the definition of miracle.
B) It is like the power itself in being non-possible (for others).
C) It means the miracle is not a true miracle.
D) It only applies to saints, not prophets.

Key Takeaway
The act of a miracle is within the prophet's potential power, but the power to perform it is beyond human capacity, preserving its miraculous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every good action you perform is enabled by Allah alone, or do you secretly attribute your successes to your own ability? How would your daily gratitude change if you internalized the lesson that even your potential to act is a gift from Him?
?
When you hear about miracles in the Quran or sunnah, do you stop to reflect that the power behind them is entirely from Allah? How can this realization shift your reliance on Him in your own life?
?
The lesson teaches that the act is within the prophet's power, but the power to perform it is not his own. In what areas of your life do you claim a power that is not yours? What would change if you lived in constant awareness that all power belongs to Allah?

Saints' Wonders and a Falsifying Miracle
Clarifies the analogy of saints' miracles and provides a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that saints' wonders are called miracles of the prophet only by analogy, and that a claimed miracle can be falsified by its own content.

Core Idea
Saints' miracles testify to the prophet; some miracles can disprove the claimant.

The text offers two important clarifications. First, the wonders (كرامات) of saints are not independent miracles but are analogically called miracles of the prophet because they confirm his truthfulness. Second, if a claimant says his miracle is reviving a dead lizard and the lizard speaks and calls him a liar, then the event does not prove his truthfulness. These points refine the theology of miracles and prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on karamat
Focus on the phrase 'بطريق التشبيه' – the relationship is analogical. Understand why saints' wonders point to the prophet.
The saint's miracle is not a proof of his own prophethood but of the prophet's truth.
2
Analyze the lizard example
Consider the hypothetical: a claimant announces a miracle, but the miracle itself declares him a liar. What does this imply?
The miracle must be consistent with the claim; otherwise, it is not a valid miracle.
3
Reflect on the implications
Both points show that the miracle is a sign that must align with the claimant's truthfulness, not an arbitrary event.
The author ends with 'تأمل' – reflection is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من للنبى عليه السلام دون غيره معجزة، فلا خلل حينئذ في العبارة ولا يخفى بعد تسليم مساعدة عبارة المصنف، لذلك المعنى أن كون الفعل مقدوراً كنفس القدرة في عدم المقدورية وأنه لا معنى للرد لكونه معجزاً على من شرط عدم مقدورية المعجزة، فتأمل. قوله : (ظاهراً على يد مدعي النبوة) وقولهم كرامة الأولياء معجزة لنبيهم إنما هو بطريق التشبيه أي هي كالمعجزات في دلالتها على صدق نبيهم . قوله : ( وهو بعد ذلك مختاراً إلخ) هذا يدل على أنه لو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي هذا الضب الميت فأحياه فنطق أنه كاذب لا يعلم به صدقه تأمل.
From, for the prophet, peace be upon him, without others, a miracle, so there is no flaw in the phrase, and it is not hidden after accepting the assistance of the author's speech, therefore the meaning is that the act being within his power is like the power itself in non-possibility, and that there is no meaning to the refutation because it is a miracle against whoever stipulated the non-possibility of the miracle, so reflect. His saying: (manifest on the hand of the claimant of prophethood) and their saying that the wonders (karamat) of saints are a miracle for their prophet is only by way of analogy, i.e., they are like miracles in their indication of the truthfulness of their prophet. His saying: (and he after that is free, etc.) this indicates that if he said: 'My miracle is that I revive this dead lizard,' and he revived it and it spoke that he is a liar, his truthfulness is not known from it; reflect.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage consists of several editorial notes. The first note defends the coherence of the earlier discussion: the fact that the act is within the prophet's power does not detract from its miraculousness, because the power itself is not within his power. The analogy is drawn between the act and the power in terms of being beyond human capacity. The second note explains a common phrase: when saints perform wonders (karamat), they are called miracles of the prophet only by analogy, because they indicate the prophet's truthfulness. The third note revisits the sixth condition: if the claimant says his miracle is reviving a dead lizard and it speaks and calls him a liar, then his truthfulness is not known; the note suggests further reflection.

Key Terms
كرامة
أولياء
تشبيه
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saints can perform miracles that prove their own sanctity independently.
Correction: Saints' wonders are only analogically called miracles; they actually point to the truth of the prophet.

Checkpoint
How are the wonders of saints related to the miracle of the prophet according to the note?
A) They are independent miracles of the saints.
B) They are called miracles of the prophet by analogy because they indicate his truthfulness.
C) They have no connection to prophethood.
D) They are stronger than the prophet's miracles.

Key Takeaway
Saints' wonders testify to the prophet's truth by analogy, and a miracle must align with the claimant's truthfulness to be valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been impressed by someone's apparent spiritual power or extraordinary signs without stopping to check whether their message and character align with truth? How will you guard your heart from being deceived by mere spectacles?
?
If a saint's wonder is only a sign of the prophet's truth, not an independent miracle, does that force you to reexamine whom you look up to? Are you more focused on the person performing wonders or on the Prophet they claim to follow?
?
The lizard spoke and discredited the claimant. Have you ever ignored an obvious contradiction in someone you admired because you wanted to believe they were right? What would it take for you to accept the evidence even if it shatters your assumption?
